        <a class="external text" href="http://w3.u-grenoble3.fr/almura/" rel="nofollow">
         ALMURA: Atlas linguistique multimédia de la région Rhône-Alpes et des régions limitrophes
        </a>
        — Multimedia website from
        <a href="/wiki/Stendhal_University" title="Stendhal University">
         Stendhal University-Grenoble 3
        </a>
        with MP3 audio clips of more than 700 words and expressions by native speakers grouped in 15 themes by village. The linguistic atlas demonstrates the transition from Franco-Provençal
        <a href="/wiki/Phonology" title="Phonology">
         phonology
        </a>
        in the north to Occitan phonology in the south. (select: ATLAS)
       </li>
       <li>
        <a class="external text" href="http://alaval.unine.ch:" rel="nofollow">
         L'Atlas linguistique audiovisuel du Valais romand
        </a>
        — Multimedia website from the
        <a href="/wiki/University_of_Neuch%C3%A2tel" title="University of Neuchâtel">
         University of Neuchâtel
        </a>
        with audio and video clips of Franco-Provençal speakers from the canton of
        <a href="/wiki/Valais" title="Valais">
         Valais
        </a>
        ,
        <a href="/wiki/Switzerland" title="Switzerland">
         Switzerland
        </a>
        .
       </li>
       <li>
        <a class="external text" href="http://www.languesdefranceenchansons.com/" rel="nofollow">
         Les Langues de France en chansons:
         <i>
          N'tra Linga e Chanfon
         </i>
        </a>
        — Multimedia website with numerous audio clips of native Franco-Provençal speakers singing traditional songs. Select:
        <i>
         TRAINS DIRECTS
        </i>
        → scroll to:
        <i>
         Francoprovençal
        </i>

      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Toponyms">
        Toponyms
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Franco-Proven%C3%A7al_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Toponyms">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <p>
       Other than in
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Family_name" title="Family name">
        family names
       </a>
       , the Franco-Provençal legacy survives primarily in
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Placename" title="Placename">
        placenames
       </a>
       . Many are immediately recognizable, ending in -az, -o(t)z, -uz, -ax, -ex, -ux, -ou(l)x, -aulx, and -ieu(x). These suffixes are vestiges of an old medieval orthographic practice indicating the stressed syllable of a word. In polysyllables, 'z' indicates a
       <a href="/wiki/Paroxytone" title="Paroxytone">
        paroxytone
       </a>
       (stress on penultimate syllable) and 'x' indicates an
       <a href="/wiki/Oxytone" title="Oxytone">
        oxytone
       </a>
       (stress on last syllable). So, Chanaz
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        <a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/French" title="Help:IPA/French">
         [/ˈʃɑ.nɑ/]
        </a>
       </span>
       (
       <i>
        <u>
         sha
        </u>
        na
       </i>
       ) but Chênex
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        <a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/French" title="Help:IPA/French">
         [/ʃɛˈne/]
        </a>
       </span>
       (
       <i>
        shè
        <u>
         né
        </u>
       </i>
       ). The following is a list of all such toponyms:
      </p>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Euntro%C3%B9.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1052" data-file-width="3297" height="70" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/54/Euntro%C3%B9.jpg/220px-Euntro%C3%B9.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/54/Euntro%C3%B9.jpg/330px-Euntro%C3%B9.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/54/Euntro%C3%B9.jpg/440px-Euntro%C3%B9.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Euntro%C3%B9.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Bilingual road sign (
         <a href="/wiki/French_language" title="French language">
          French
         </a>
         -
         <a href="/wiki/Vald%C3%B4tain_dialect" title="Valdôtain dialect">
          Valdôtain
         </a>
         ) in
         <a href="/wiki/Introd" title="Introd">
          Introd
         </a>
         ,
         <a href="/wiki/Aosta_Valley" title="Aosta Valley">
          Aosta Valley
         </a>
         .
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:182px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Charvex-sign2.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="260" data-file-width="230" height="203" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7b/Charvex-sign2.jpg/180px-Charvex-sign2.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/7/7b/Charvex-sign2.jpg 1.5x" width="180"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Charvex-sign2.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Road sign for Charvex (La Balme de Thuy), Haute-Savoie, France after a name change in the 1990s to a historical
         <a href="/wiki/Savoy" title="Savoy">
          Savoyard
         </a>
         spelling. (Former village name: Charvet.)
        </div>
       </div>

      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Literature">
        Literature
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Franco-Proven%C3%A7al_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Literature">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:142px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Noues-III.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="800" data-file-width="475" height="236" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/Noues-III.jpg/140px-Noues-III.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/Noues-III.jpg/210px-Noues-III.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/Noues-III.jpg/280px-Noues-III.jpg 2x" width="140"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Noues-III.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Text of a carol about the appearance of a comet in 1682 by Jean Chapelon.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       A long tradition of Franco-Provençal literature exists, although no prevailing written form of the language has materialized. An early 12th century fragment containing 105 verses from a poem about
       <a href="/wiki/Alexander_the_Great" title="Alexander the Great">
        Alexander the Great
       </a>
       may be the earliest known work in the language.
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Girart_de_Roussillon" title="Girart de Roussillon">
         Girart de Roussillon
        </a>
       </i>
       , an epic with 10,002 lines from the mid-12th century,
       <sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        [
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Girart_de_Roussillon#Romance" title="Girart de Roussillon">
          <span title="This text contradicts material at the article 'Girart de Roussillon#Romance'. (February 2014)">
           contradictory
          </span>
         </a>
        </i>
        ]
       </sup>
       has been asserted to be Franco-Provençal. It certainly contains prominent Franco-Provençal features, although the editor of an authoritative edition of this work claims that the language is a mixture of French and Occitan forms.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29">
        <a href="#cite_note-29">
         [29]
        </a>
       </sup>
       A significant document from the same period containing a list of vassals in the County of Forez also is not without literary value.
      </p>
      <p>
       Among the first historical writings in Franco-Provençal are legal texts by
       <a href="/wiki/Civil_law_notary" title="Civil law notary">
        civil law notaries
       </a>
       that appeared in the 13th century as Latin was being abandoned for official administration. These include a translation of the
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Corpus_Juris_Civilis" title="Corpus Juris Civilis">
         Corpus Juris Civilis
        </a>
       </i>
       (known as the
       <i>
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Justinian_Code" title="Justinian Code">
         Justinian Code
        </a>
       </i>
       ) in the vernacular spoken in Grenoble. Religious works also were translated and conceived in Franco-Provençal dialects at some monasteries in the region.
       <i>
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bartholomew" title="Bartholomew">
         The Legend of Saint Bartholomew
        </a>
       </i>
       is one such work that survives in Lyonnais patois from the 13th century.
      </p>
      <p>
       <a href="/wiki/Marguerite_d%27Oingt" title="Marguerite d'Oingt">
        Marguerite d'Oingt
       </a>
       (ca. 1240–1310),
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Prioress" title="Prioress">
        prioress
       </a>
       of a
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Carthusian" title="Carthusian">
        Carthusian
       </a>
       nunnery near
       <a href="/wiki/Mionnay" title="Mionnay">
        Mionnay
       </a>
       (France), composed two remarkable sacred texts in her native Lyonnais dialect, in addition to her writings in Latin. The first, entitled
       <i>
        Speculum
       </i>
       ("The Mirror"), describes three
       <a href="/wiki/Miracle" title="Miracle">
        miraculous
       </a>
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vision_(religion)" title="Vision (religion)">
        visions
       </a>
       and their meanings. The other work,
       <i>
        Li Via seiti Biatrix, virgina de Ornaciu
       </i>
       ("The Life of the Blessed Virgin
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Beatrix_d%27Ornacieux" title="Beatrix d'Ornacieux">
        Beatrix d'Ornacieux
       </a>
       "), is a long biography of a
       <a href="/wiki/Nun" title="Nun">
        nun
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Mysticism" title="Mysticism">
        mystic
       </a>
       consecrated to the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Passion_(Christianity)" title="Passion (Christianity)">
        Passion
       </a>
       whose faith lead to a devout cult. This text contributed to the
       <a href="/wiki/Beatification" title="Beatification">
        beatification
       </a>
       of the nun more than 500 years later by
       <a href="/wiki/Pope_Pius_IX" title="Pope Pius IX">
        Pope Pius IX
       </a>
       in 1869.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30">
        <a href="#cite_note-30">
         [30]
        </a>
       </sup>
       A line from the work in her dialect follows:
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31">
        <a href="#cite_note-31">
         [31]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <dl>
       <dd>
        § 112 : «
        <i>
         Quant vit co li diz vicayros que ay o coventavet fayre, ce alyet cela part et en ot mout de dongiers et de travayl, ancis que cil qui gardont lo lua d'Emuet li volissant layssyer co que il demandavet et que li evesques de Valenci o volit commandar. Totes veys yses com Deus o aveyt ordonat oy se fit.
        </i>
        »
       </dd>
      </dl>
      <p>
       Religious conflicts in
       <a href="/wiki/Geneva" title="Geneva">
        Geneva
       </a>
       between
       <a href="/wiki/Calvinism" title="Calvinism">
        Calvinist
       </a>
       Reformers and staunch
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Catholics" title="Catholics">
        Catholics
       </a>
       , supported by the Duchy of Savoy, brought forth many texts in Franco-Provençal during the early 17th century. One of the best known is
       <i>
        Cé qu'è lainô
       </i>
       ("The One Above"), which was composed by an unknown writer in 1603. The long narrative poem describes
       <a href="/wiki/L%27Escalade" title="L'Escalade">
        l'Escalade
       </a>
       , a raid by the Savoyard army that generated patriotic sentiments. It became the unofficial national anthem of the
       <a href="/wiki/Canton_of_Geneva" title="Canton of Geneva">
        Republic of Geneva
       </a>
       . The first three verses follow below (in
       <a href="/wiki/Geneva" title="Geneva">
        Genevois
       </a>
       dialect)
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32">
        <a href="#cite_note-32">
         [32]
        </a>
       </sup>
       with a translation:
      </p>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:162px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Cequelaino.png">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="946" data-file-width="664" height="228" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/Cequelaino.png/160px-Cequelaino.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/Cequelaino.png/240px-Cequelaino.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/Cequelaino.png/320px-Cequelaino.png 2x" width="160"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Cequelaino.png" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         <i>
          Cé qu'è l'ainô
         </i>
         musical score showing verses 1, 2, 4, &amp; 68.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <center>
       <table>
        <tr>
         <td>
          <dl>
           <dd>
            <i>
             Cé qu'è lainô, le Maitre dé bataille,
            </i>
           </dd>
           <dd>
            <i>
             Que se moqué et se ri dé canaille;
            </i>
           </dd>
           <dd>
            <i>
             A bin fai vi, pè on desande nai,
            </i>
           </dd>
           <dd>
            <i>
             Qu'il étivé patron dé Genevoi.
            </i>
           </dd>
          </dl>
         </td>
         <td>
          <dl>
           <dd>
            The One above, the Master of the battles,
           </dd>
           <dd>
            Who mock and laugh at the rabble,
           </dd>
           <dd>
            Made them see well, on a Saturday night,
           </dd>
           <dd>
            That He was protector of the Genevese people.
           </dd>
          </dl>
         </td>
        </tr>
        <tr>
         <td>
          <dl>
           <dd>
            <i>
             I son vegnu le doze de dessanbro
            </i>
           </dd>
           <dd>
            <i>
             Pè onna nai asse naire que d'ancro;
            </i>
           </dd>
           <dd>
            <i>
             Y étivé l'an mil si san et dou,
            </i>
           </dd>
           <dd>
            <i>
             Qu'i veniron parla ou pou troi tou.
            </i>
           </dd>
          </dl>
         </td>
         <td>
          <dl>
           <dd>
            They came on the twelfth of December,
           </dd>
           <dd>
            On a night as black as ink;
           </dd>
           <dd>
            It was the year sixteen-hundred-and-two,
           </dd>
           <dd>
            That they speak of, at the earliest (hour).
           </dd>
          </dl>
         </td>
        </tr>
        <tr>
         <td>
          <dl>
           <dd>
            <i>
             Pè onna nai qu'étive la pe naire
            </i>
           </dd>
           <dd>
            <i>
             I veniron; y n'étai pas pè bairè;
            </i>
           </dd>
           <dd>
            <i>
             Y étivé pè pilli nou maison,
            </i>
           </dd>
           <dd>
            <i>
             Et no tüa sans aucuna raison.
            </i>
           </dd>
          </dl>
         </td>
         <td>
          <dl>
           <dd>
            On the blackest night
           </dd>
           <dd>
            They came — it was not for drinking —
           </dd>
           <dd>
            To plunder our houses,
           </dd>
           <dd>
            And to kill us without any reason.
           </dd>
          </dl>
         </td>
        </tr>
       </table>
      </center>
      <p>
       Several writers created
       <a href="/wiki/Satire" title="Satire">
        satirical
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Morality" title="Morality">
        moralistic
       </a>
       ,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Poem" title="Poem">
        poetic
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Comedy" title="Comedy">
        comic
       </a>
       , and
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Theater" title="Theater">
        theatrical
       </a>
       texts during the era that followed, which indicates the vitality of the language at that time. These include:
       <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Bernardin_Uchard&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Bernardin Uchard (page does not exist)">
        Bernardin Uchard
       </a>
       (1575–1624),
       <a href="/wiki/Author" title="Author">
        author
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Playwright" title="Playwright">
        playwright
       </a>
       from
       <a href="/wiki/Bresse" title="Bresse">
        Bresse
       </a>
       ;
       <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Henri_Perrin&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Henri Perrin (page does not exist)">
        Henri Perrin
       </a>
       , comic playwright from Lyon;
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jean_Millet" title="Jean Millet">
        Jean Millet
       </a>
       (1600?–1675), author of
       <a href="/wiki/Pastoral" title="Pastoral">
        pastorals
       </a>
       , poems, and comedies from Grenoble;
       <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Jacques_Brossard_de_Montaney&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Jacques Brossard de Montaney (page does not exist)">
        Jacques Brossard de Montaney
       </a>
       (1638–1702), writer of comedies and
       <a href="/wiki/Carol_(music)" title="Carol (music)">
        carols
       </a>
       from Bresse;
       <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Jean_Chapelon&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Jean Chapelon (page does not exist)">
        Jean Chapelon
       </a>
       (1647–1694), priest and composer of more than 1,500 carols, songs, epistles, and essays from
       <a href="/wiki/Saint-%C3%89tienne" title="Saint-Étienne">
        Saint-Étienne
       </a>
       ; and
       <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Fran%C3%A7ois_Blanc_dit_la_Goutte&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="François Blanc dit la Goutte (page does not exist)">
        François Blanc dit la Goutte
       </a>
       (1690–1742), writer of
       <a href="/wiki/Prose" title="Prose">
        prose
       </a>
       poems, including
       <i>
        Grenoblo maléirou
       </i>
       about the great flood of 1733 in Grenoble. 19th century authors include
       <a href="/wiki/Guillaume_Roquille" title="Guillaume Roquille">
        Guillaume Roquille
       </a>
       (1804–1860), working-class poet from Rive-de-Gier near Saint-Chamond,
       <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Joseph_B%C3%A9ard_dit_l%27%C3%89clair&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Joseph Béard dit l'Éclair (page does not exist)">
        Joseph Béard dit l'Éclair
       </a>
       (1805–1872), physician, poet, and songwriter from Rumilly, and
       <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Louis_Bornet&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Louis Bornet (page does not exist)">
        Louis Bornet
       </a>
       (1818–1880) of Gruyères.
       <a href="/wiki/Clair_Tisseur" title="Clair Tisseur">
        Clair Tisseur
       </a>
       (1827–1896), architect of
       <a href="/wiki/%C3%89glise_du_Bon-Pasteur" title="Église du Bon-Pasteur">
        Bon-Pasteur Church
       </a>
       in Lyon, published many writings under the pen name "Nizier du Puitspelu". These include a popular dictionary and humorous works in Lyonnaise dialect that have reprinted for more than 100 years.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33">
        <a href="#cite_note-33">
         [33]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <div class="thumb tleft">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:112px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Amelie_Gex_2.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="300" data-file-width="230" height="143" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/Amelie_Gex_2.jpg/110px-Amelie_Gex_2.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/Amelie_Gex_2.jpg/165px-Amelie_Gex_2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/Amelie_Gex_2.jpg/220px-Amelie_Gex_2.jpg 2x" width="110"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Amelie_Gex_2.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         <a href="/wiki/Am%C3%A9lie_Gex" title="Amélie Gex">
          Amélie Gex
         </a>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:122px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Jean-Baptiste_Cerlogne.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="300" data-file-width="230" height="157" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/38/Jean-Baptiste_Cerlogne.jpg/120px-Jean-Baptiste_Cerlogne.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/38/Jean-Baptiste_Cerlogne.jpg/180px-Jean-Baptiste_Cerlogne.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/3/38/Jean-Baptiste_Cerlogne.jpg 2x" width="120"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Jean-Baptiste_Cerlogne.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         <a href="/wiki/Jean-Baptiste_Cerlogne" title="Jean-Baptiste Cerlogne">
          Jean-Baptiste Cerlogne
         </a>
         originating from
         <a href="/wiki/Saint-Nicolas,_Aosta_Valley" title="Saint-Nicolas, Aosta Valley">
          Saint-Nicolas, Aosta Valley
         </a>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       <a href="/wiki/Am%C3%A9lie_Gex" title="Amélie Gex">
        Amélie Gex
       </a>
       (1835–1883) wrote in her native
       <i>
        patois
       </i>
       as well as
       <a href="/wiki/French_language" title="French language">
        French
       </a>
       . She was a passionate advocate for her language. Her literary efforts encompassed lyrical themes, work, love, tragic loss, nature, the passing of time, religion, and politics, and are considered by many to be the most significant contributions to the literature. Among her works are:
       <i>
        Reclans de Savoué
       </i>
       ("Echos from Savoy", 1879),
       <i>
        Lo cent ditons de Pierre d'Emo
       </i>
       ("One Hundred Sayings by Pierre du Bon-Sens", 1879),
       <i>
        Poesies
       </i>
       ("Poems", 1880),
       <i>
        Vieilles gens et vieilles choses: Histoires de ma rue et de mon village
       </i>
       ("Old people and old things: Stories from my street and from my village", 1889),
       <i>
        Fables
       </i>
       (1898), and
       <i>
        Contio de la Bova
       </i>
       ("Tales from the Cowshed").
      </p>
      <p>
       The writings of the
       <i>
        abbé
       </i>
       <a href="/wiki/Jean-Baptiste_Cerlogne" title="Jean-Baptiste Cerlogne">
        Jean-Baptiste Cerlogne
       </a>
       (1826–1910) are credited with reestablishing the cultural identity of the Aosta Valley. His early poetry includes:
       <i>
        L'infan prodeggo
       </i>
       (1855),
       <i>
        Marenda a Tsesalet
       </i>
       (1856) and
       <i>
        La bataille di vatse a Vertosan
       </i>
       (1858); among his scholarly works are:
       <i>
        Petite grammaire du dialecte valdotain
       </i>
       (1893),
       <i>
        Dictionnaire du dialecte valdôtain
       </i>
       (1908) and
       <i>
        Le patois valdotain: son origine litéraire et sa graphie
       </i>
       (1909). The
       <a class="external text" href="http://www.regione.vda.it/cultura/eventi/concours_cerlogne/default_i.asp" rel="nofollow">
        Concours Cerlogne
       </a>
       – an annual event named in his honor – has focused thousands of Italian students on preserving the region's language, literature, and heritage since 1963.
      </p>
      <p>
       At the end of the 19th century, regional dialects of Franco-Provençal were disappearing due to the expansion of the French language into all walks of life and the emigration of rural people to urban centers. Cultural and regional savant societies began to collect oral
       <a href="/wiki/Folklore" title="Folklore">
        folk tales
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Proverb" title="Proverb">
        proverbs
       </a>
       , and
       <a href="/wiki/Legend" title="Legend">
        legends
       </a>
       from native speakers in an effort that continues to today. Numerous works have been published.
      </p>
      <p>
       <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Prosper_Convert&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Prosper Convert (page does not exist)">
        Prosper Convert
       </a>
       (1852–1934), the bard of Bresse;
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Louis_Mercier" title="Louis Mercier">
        Louis Mercier
       </a>
       (1870–1951),
       <a href="/wiki/Folk_music" title="Folk music">
        folk singer
       </a>
       and author of more than twelve volumes of prose from Coutouvre near
       <a href="/wiki/Roanne" title="Roanne">
        Roanne
       </a>
       ;
       <a href="/wiki/Just_Songeon" title="Just Songeon">
        Just Songeon
       </a>
       (1880–1940), author, poet, and activist from La Combe, Sillingy near
       <a href="/wiki/Annecy" title="Annecy">
        Annecy
       </a>
       ;
       <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Eug%C3%A9nie_Martinet&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Eugénie Martinet (page does not exist)">
        Eugénie Martinet
       </a>
       (1896–1983), poet from
       <a href="/wiki/Aosta" title="Aosta">
        Aosta
       </a>
       ; and
       <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Joseph_Yerly&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Joseph Yerly (page does not exist)">
        Joseph Yerly
       </a>
       (1896–1961) of
       <a href="/wiki/Gruy%C3%A8res" title="Gruyères">
        Gruyères
       </a>
       whose complete works were published in
       <i>
        Kan la têra tsantè
       </i>
       ("When the earth sang"), are well known for their use of patois in the 20th century. Louis des Ambrois de Nevache, from Upper Susa Valley, transcribed popular songs and wrote some original poetry in local patois. There are compositions in the current language on the album Enfestar, an artistic project from Piedmont
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34">
        <a href="#cite_note-34">
         [34]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The first comic book in a Franco-Provençal dialect,
       <i>
        Le rebloshon que tyouè!
       </i>
       ("The cheese that killed!"), from the
       <i>
        Fanfoué des Pnottas
       </i>
       series by Félix Meynet, appeared in 2000.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35">
        <a href="#cite_note-35">
         [35]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Two popular works from
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/The_Adventures_of_Tintin" title="The Adventures of Tintin">
         The Adventures of Tintin
        </a>
       </i>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36">
        <a href="#cite_note-36">
         [36]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37">
        <a href="#cite_note-37">
         [37]
        </a>
       </sup>
       and one from the
       <a href="/wiki/Lucky_Luke" title="Lucky Luke">
        Lucky Luke
       </a>
       series
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38">
        <a href="#cite_note-38">
         [38]
        </a>
       </sup>
       were published in Franco-Provençal translations for young readers in 2006 and 2007.
      </p>

         <td class="navbox-abovebelow" colspan="2">
          <div>
           <div class="hlist hlist-separated">
            <ul>
             <li>
              <i>
               Italics
              </i>
              indicate
              <a href="/wiki/Language_death" title="Language death">
               extinct languages
              </a>
             </li>
             <li>
              <b>
               Bold
              </b>
              indicates
              <a href="/wiki/List_of_languages_by_number_of_native_speakers" title="List of languages by number of native speakers">
               languages with more than 5 million speakers
              </a>
             </li>
             <li>
              Languages between parentheses are
              <a href="/wiki/Variety_(linguistics)" title="Variety (linguistics)">
               varieties
              </a>
              of the language on their left.
             </li>
            </ul>
           </div>

        <tr>
         <th class="navbox-group" scope="row" style="width:1%">
          <a href="/wiki/Help:Authority_control" title="Help:Authority control">
           Authority control
          </a>
         </th>
         <td class="navbox-list navbox-odd" style="text-align:left;border-left-width:2px;border-left-style:solid;width:100%;padding:0px">
          <div style="padding:0em 0.25em">
           <ul>
